        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send

            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '0f6d7aed-2341-4b4c-b963-11d9ae552e3f'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d11373007" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> demonstrates high energy density owing to its high specific capacity and high discharge voltage, but suffering from rapid performance decay due to severe structural degradation and aggravated surface side reaction during high-voltage cycling. Herein, a Mg/Zr modified Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.25</sub>Mg<sub>0.08</sub>Mn<sub>0.64</sub>Zr<sub>0.03</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode is proposed with good structural stability and excellent cycling performance, showing reversible capacity of 123.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (0.1 C) and capacity retention of 99.1% after 100 cycles. The cycling stability is outstanding among P2-type cathodes reported so far. In situ XRD analyses reveal a suppressed P2-O2 phase transition after Mg modification, further incorporation of Zr greatly stabilizes the layered structure as some Zr ions reside in the Na layer providing a pinning effect to reduce the slide of TMO<sub>2</sub> layer. First-principles calculations suggest that oxygen loss in Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.25</sub>Mg<sub>0.08</sub>Mn<sub>0.64</sub>Zr<sub>0.03</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode is suppressed as Zr incorporation prevents the formation of oxygen vacancies. XPS analyses verify a Zr─O protective layer self-segregated on particle surface during material synthesis. The expanded Na<sup>+</sup> transport channel confirmed by the XRD analysis well explains the favored Na-ion mobility verified by a high reversible capacity of 105.5 mAh/g at 20 C. This work sheds new light on providing a practical P2-structured cathode material for high-power sodium ion batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202411347-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202411347-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) with high energy density and long cycling life have been the best choice for electronic devices and electric vehicles (EV).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0001_R_d11372999e524" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Mater. Chem. Soc. Rev. Joule Adv. Mater. Energy Environ. Sci.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the rapid increasing demand for LIBs causes worry about the limit lithium resources. Therefore, exploration of alternatives to LIBs has been enforced in recent years. Sodium ion batteries (SIBs) have been considered as a promising candidate due to the abundant sodium resources and similar reaction mechanism with LIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0002_R_d11372999e529" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Energy Mater. Energy Storage Mater. Nat. Rev. Mater. Chem. Soc. Rev.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> While hard carbon is the only optimal choice of anode material for SIBs,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0003_R_d11372999e534" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Energy Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> there are more choices for cathode materials. The most commonly investigated sodium cathode materials include polyanion materials,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0004_R_d11372999e540" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Funct. Mater. Small Nano Energy Nano Energy Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Prussian Blue analogs,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0005_R_d11372999e545" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Funct. Mater. J. Mater. Chem. A ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and layered oxide materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0006_R_d11372999e551" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Mater. Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Mater. Energy Storage Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Among the widely investigated cathode materials, the polyanion materials and Prussian Blue analogs have disadvantage of low volumetric energy density and low electronic conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0007_R_d11372999e558" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Energy Storage Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In comparison, the layer oxide cathodes Na<sub>x</sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> (Na<sub>x</sub>TMO<sub>2</sub>, x ≤ 1, TM = Fe, Co, Ni, Mn, Cu, etc.) have the advantage of high compacted density, high conductivity, and easy synthesis, which have attracted much attention.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0008_R_d11372999e572" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Layered Na<sub>x</sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> materials can be classified into two main structural groups: P2 and O3 types, where sodium ions tend to accommodate at prismatic and octahedral sites between the adjacent TMO<sub>2</sub> layers, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0009_R_d11372999e584" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Physica B+C">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The O3 type layer oxides possess a stable structure during Na<sup>+</sup> ions insertion and desertion in low voltage range of 2.0–4.0&nbsp;V and delivering a high reversible capacity ≈145 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0010_R_d11372999e593" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Small">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the humid air instability, lower operating voltage with complicate shape of discharge voltage profile, and inferior rate performance have largely plagued their practical application.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0011_R_d11372999e599" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Energy Environ. Sci. ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> On the other hand, P2-type layered oxides, typically Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, show a high operation voltage (≈3.7&nbsp;V), high theoretical capacity (≈173 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), excellent air stability and rate performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0008_R_d11372999e615" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a>, <a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0012_R_d11372999e615" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> All these merits combined with the superior low-temperature performance make P2 sodium layered oxides promising cathode candidates for high-end SIBs those applied in power supplies for the electric vehicles, portable electric tools, and forklifts especially in cold area. Unfortunately, the fact is that the P2-Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> suffers from severe capacity degradation even during initial cycling due to irreversible P2-O2 phase transition when charged to above 4.2&nbsp;V which causes lattice distortions and intragranular cracking.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0013_R_d11372999e629" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - J. Energy Chem.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the side reaction on the electrode surface at high voltage leads to consumption of electrolyte, TM dissolution, and formation of high resistance CEI film. Before the high power P2 type layered oxide materials can be successfully introduced in commercial application, chemical and structural modifications of these materials are necessary to obtain stable cycling performance.</p>
         
         <p>To improve the cycling stability of P2 type layered oxide materials, many reports have focused on alleviating the phase transition by doping different metal elements, such as Li,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0006_R_d11372999e637" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Mater. Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Mater. Energy Storage Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater.">6</a>, <a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0014_R_d11372999e637" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Mg,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0015_R_d11372999e642" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Al,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0016_R_d11372999e647" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Zn,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0017_R_d11372999e653" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Power Sources">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Fe,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0018_R_d11372999e658" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Cu,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0019_R_d11372999e664" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Chem. Eng. J.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Ca,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0020_R_d11372999e669" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Ti,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0021_R_d11372999e674" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Sci. Adv.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Sn<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0022_R_d11372999e680" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nano Lett.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> et&nbsp;al. Surface protective layers, such as Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0023_R_d11372999e692" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0024_R_d11372999e701" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> ZnO,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0025_R_d11372999e707" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Phys. Chem. C">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and NaPO<sub>3</sub><sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0026_R_d11372999e713" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> have also been applied to suppress the interphase side-reactions during cycling. However, the practical cycling performances of these modified materials are still not satisfying.</p>
         
         <p>It is worth mentioning that Mg<sup>2+</sup> doped P2-Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> materials have been well investigated which show obviously suppressed P2-O2 phase transition.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0015_R_d11372999e732" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">15</a>, <a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0027_R_d11372999e732" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Energy Environ. Sci. Chem. Mater. Inorg. Chem. J. Mater. Chem. A Adv. Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Y. G. Guo's group<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0015_R_d11372999e737" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> indicated that the inactive Mg<sup>2+</sup> ions in the TM layers allow more Na<sup>+</sup> ions to reside in the prismatic sites upon charging. The P2–O2 phase transition and the Na <sup>+</sup>/vacancy ordering in Na<sub>0.67</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.33</sub>O<sub>2</sub> are both effectively inhibited during charging and discharging. Peter G. Bruce et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0027_R_d11372999e758" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Energy Environ. Sci. Chem. Mater. Inorg. Chem. J. Mater. Chem. A Adv. Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> also performed structural analysis of P2-type Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3-x</sub>Mg<sub>x</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (0 &lt;&nbsp;x&nbsp;&lt; 0.2) using neutron diffraction and confirmed that Mg<sup>2+</sup> substituted at Ni<sup>2+</sup> sites from ordered [(Ni<sup>2+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup>)O<sub>6</sub>] honeycomb units along the ab-plane. The Mg<sup>2+</sup> substituted samples showed enhanced cycling stability due to the suppression of the O2 phase and switch to the formation of an OP4 phase. Some other literatures also reported the improved cycling stability of Mg substituted Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> samples.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0027_R_d11372999e796" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Energy Environ. Sci. Chem. Mater. Inorg. Chem. J. Mater. Chem. A Adv. Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, all these literatures only provided stable cycling data of 100 cycles, no long-term stable performance was reported. In order to achieve practical application, the Mg modified Na<sub>0.67</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.33</sub>O<sub>2</sub> materials need further chemical and structural modulation.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, we design and synthesize a Mg/Zr co-substituted P2-type layered oxide Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.25</sub>Mg<sub>0.08</sub>Mn<sub>0.64</sub>Zr<sub>0.03</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (denoted as NMMZ3). Mg/Zr co-substitution achieves a robust bulk structure, furthermore, a self-segregated Zr─O shell formed on the particle surface providing a protective layer against the electrolyte corrosion. The synergetic effect of robust bulk structure and self-formed corrosion-resisting surface attribute to the excellent cycling stability of NMMZ3. Herein, the effects of Mg/Zr co-substitution on electrochemical properties, structure stability, and humidity endurance have been systematically investigated. The synthetic process and function mechanism are detailly shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202411347-fig-0001">1</a>. The obtained NMMZ3 cathode exhibits not only fast charge/discharge capability but also long-cycling life with excellent structural stability (no cracks after 500 cycles). This electrode also shows unexceptionable cycling properties even at higher cut-off voltage of 4.6&nbsp;V. More importantly, the NMMZ3 cathode shows stable electrochemical performance in a wide temperature range from −25 to 55&nbsp;°C and possesses enhanced air and thermodynamic stability. The superior electrochemical performance of NMMZ3 indicates that this P2-type layered oxide material is a promising cathode candidate for practical application.</p>

      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202411347-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202411347-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202411347-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202411347-sec-0030-title">2.1 Material Characterization and Crystal Structure</h3>
            
            <p>A series of Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.25</sub>Mg<sub>0.08</sub>Mn<sub>0.67-x/100</sub>Zr<sub>x/100</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (simply denoted as NMMZx) (x = 0,1,3,5) materials were synthesized through a co-precipitation method combined with solid calcination. The results of Inductively Coupled Plasma-Atomic Emission Spectrometry (ICP-AES) show that the atomic ratios of metal elements in all samples are close to the aimed stoichiometry (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information) compares the cycling performance of these samples at charge/discharge current density of 1 C rate (130&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>). It is found that proper amount of Mg/Zr co-substitution greatly enhanced the electrochemical performance of Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> material. The NMMZ3 sample showed the best cycling performance among all the prepared samples with a reversible capacity of 116.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1 C and capacity retention of 97.5% after 100 cycles. In comparison, the NMM sample showed a reversible capacity of 114.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and capacity retention of 82.7% after 100 cycles. To clarify the remarkable effect of Mg/Zr co-substitution in the electrochemical performance of Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> material, the bulk crystalline structure and surface composition of the NMMZ3 were detailedly analyzed as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202411347-fig-0002">2</a>. The powder X-ray patterns in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411347-fig-0002">2a</a> show that all the characteristic peaks of NMMZ3 and NMM are sharp and clear, and can be indexed into a typical hexagonal P2-structure with <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/mmc symmetry. Furthermore, all the peaks of NMMZ3 display lower angle positions compared to the corresponding peaks of NMM. The detailed results of Rietveld refinements presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> and Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information) indicate an expanded lattice caused by Zr incorporation. The lattice parameters expand from a = b = 2.8929 Å, c = 11.1715 Å (NMM) to a = b = 2.8935 Å, c = 11.1740 Å (NMMZ3) (See Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information), and the sodium layer d<sub>O-Na-O</sub> spacing also increases from 3.4855 to 3.4863 Å (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). These results demonstrate the successful introduction of larger-size Zr<sup>4+</sup> cations into the lattice structure, and the expanded layered structure may enhance the de/intercalation capabilities of Na<sup>+</sup>.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202411347-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a63b0bc2-cd5d-4402-bc67-ba9c80387534/adfm202411347-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/a63b0bc2-cd5d-4402-bc67-ba9c80387534/adfm202411347-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9c47a9a4-a01c-40e1-bfef-a237f41e2b1d/adfm202411347-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a63b0bc2-cd5d-4402-bc67-ba9c80387534/adfm202411347-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202411347-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202411347" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Powder XRD patterns of NMM and NMMZ3 samples. The inset shows an enlargement with in the 2-theta range of 14°–18° and 30°–50°. b) Atomic percentage of TMs at different etching depths. c) Formation energy and lattice diagram of Zr ion in Na layer and TM layer. d) SEM image, e,f) HRTEM images of NMM sample. g) SEM image, h,i) HRTEM images, and j) EDS mapping of NMMZ3 sample.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>It is noticed that trace characteristic peaks of ZrO<sub>2</sub> also appear in the XRD pattern of NMMZ3 sample. Since the carbonate precursor of NMMZ3 possesses a uniform distribution of all elements (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information), it is speculated that some Zr<sup>4+</sup> ions migrated to the particle surface during sintering process forming a Zr─O segregation layer. To further clarify the influence of Zr incorporation in the material microstructure, SEM/STEM observations and XPS surface analyses were performed. The SEM images of the NMM and NMMZ3 particles shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411347-fig-0002">2d,g</a> display typical polycrystalline morphologies. Energy Dispersive Spectrometer (STEM-EDS) mappings in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411347-fig-0002">2j</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information) prove that Na, Ni, Mn, and Mg elements distribute uniformly in whole NMM and NMMZ3 particles. For the Zr element, besides distributing in the whole particle some Zr rich phase is also observed. XPS etching analysis demonstrates the elemental compositions of NMMZ3 from surface to bulk phase in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411347-fig-0002">2b</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information). It is found that the Zr content decreased with the increase of etching depth, while the other elements show increase content when etching deeper. The valence state of Zr was +4 at all etching sites (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0028_R_d11372999e993" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Electrochim. Acta ChemElectroChem">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The XPS results indicate a segregated Zr─O layer on the particle surface. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information) further shows the EDS mapping results on the cross-section of a cut NMMZ3 particle, which further demonstrate that Zr ions also have been integrated into the crystal structure.</p>
            
            <p>High Angle Annular Dark-Field Scanning Transmission Electron Microscopy (HAADF-STEM) observation of crystal structure was performed as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411347-fig-0002">2e,f,h,i</a> for NMM and NMMZ3, respectively. It can be seen that the space distance of the lattice fringes for NMMZ3 is 0.578&nbsp;nm larger than the 0.503&nbsp;nm of NMM, which is well consistent with the results of XRD Rietveld refinement. Moreover, it is founded that the atom arrangement in the surface region (with a thickness of 5–10&nbsp;nm) of NMMZ3 is different from that of bulk region (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411347-fig-0002">2h</a>), while the atom arrangement in the surface and bulk of NMM (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411347-fig-0002">2e</a>) is the same. This observation further confirms that there is a cation segregation layer of ZrO<sub>2</sub> on the surface of NMMZ3 particle. It is known that the image contrast of HAADF-STEM is related to the atomic number Z, thus the heavy TM can be detected.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0029_R_d11372999e1016" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Nature">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411347-fig-0002">2f,i</a>, and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> (Supporting Information) no TM signal was observed in sodium layer of NMM sample, however TM signals can be clearly detected in the sodium layer of NMMZ3 sample. It is supposed that some Zr<sup>4+</sup> ions reside in Na layers, which may act as “pillars” to buffer the O─O repulsion at the deep de-sodiated state and suppress the P2-O2 phase transition. Density functional theory calculations were performed to illustrate the formation energies of Zr substituted at different layer positions. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411347-fig-0002">2c</a>, two Zr-substituted NMM structure models are built where Zr ions are located at the Na-sites and TM-sites, respectively. The formation energies of Zr at the Na-sites is calculated to be -234.8 and -232.5&nbsp;eV at the TM-sites, respectively. This DFT calculate results indicate that Zr is possible to reside in the Na layer. Both Zr pinning and Zr─O surface shell are expected to be beneficial to the structural stability.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202411347-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202411347-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Performance</h3>
            
            <p>The electrochemical performance of NMM and NMMZ3 were detailedly analyzed in half cells. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) measurements were conducted to compare the redox behaviors of the NMM and NMMZ3 at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV/s. Similar to many previously reported P2-typed materials,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0006_R_d11372999e1042" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Mater. Energy Storage Mater. Adv. Mater. Energy Storage Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater.">6</a>, <a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0015_R_d11372999e1042" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">15</a>, <a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0018_R_d11372999e1042" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">18</a>, <a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0019_R_d11372999e1042" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Chem. Eng. J.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> both NMM and NMMZ3 show multiple pairs of redox peaks, corresponding to a series of charge transfer reactions (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). The pairs of anodic/cathodic peaks between 2.8 and 4.0&nbsp;V can be attributed to Ni<sup>2+/3+/4+</sup> redox reactions. The redox peaks at high voltages between 3.9 and 4.3&nbsp;V is associated with Ni<sup>3+/4+</sup> and oxygen anionic redox reactions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0030_R_d11372999e1055" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Nat. Commun.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The electronic and chemical states of the Ni ions in the charge/discharge process of the samples were characterized through soft X-ray absorption spectroscopy (sXAS) shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information). It is obvious that both samples show the similar charge transfer mechanism. However, the comparison of the CV curves in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202411347-fig-0003">3a</a> clearly shows that the NMMZ3 sample displays narrower peak separations with sharper peak shape, indicating an easier Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and extraction behavior of the NMMZ3.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202411347-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/fe4bdaf9-1e6d-4f3a-9ccf-fff0059efb4e/adfm202411347-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/fe4bdaf9-1e6d-4f3a-9ccf-fff0059efb4e/adfm202411347-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5f907348-186a-4bc9-bd52-0c06ae291616/adfm202411347-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/fe4bdaf9-1e6d-4f3a-9ccf-fff0059efb4e/adfm202411347-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202411347-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202411347" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV curves of NMM and NMMZ3 at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. b) Rate performance comparison of NMM and NMMZ3. c,d) Charge and discharge profiles of NMM and NMMZ3 at various rate. e) Charge and discharge profiles of NMMZ3 at various charging rate and at 0.5 C discharging rate. f,g) Long-term cycling performance of NMM and NMMZ3 at 0.1 C and 1 C rate. h, i) Charge and discharge profiles at different cycle number.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The rate performance of the NMM and NMMZ3 electrodes were further assessed at different current densities from 0.1 C to 20 C. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411347-fig-0003">3b–d</a>, the discharge capacities of the samples decrease with the increase of current densities. The capacity of NMM at 0.1 C rate was 120.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and reduced to 87.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 C. The NMMZ3 electrode exhibited better rate performance than NMM, which showed a initial discharge capacity of 123.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with energy density of 440&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;Kg<sup>−1</sup> (for cathode active material) at 0.1 C and still delivered a high specific capacity of 105.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at high charge/discharge rate of 20 C (85.4 %&nbsp;vs that of 0.1 C). When the current density returned to 0.1 C, the discharge capacity of NMMZ3 can be recovered to 123.2&nbsp;mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, indicating a fast Na<sup>+</sup> transfer rate and strong structural stability of NMMZ3. To intuitively describe the fast charge capability of NMMZ3, the cells were charged at different rates (from 0.5 C to 20 C) and discharged at the same current density of 0.5 C rate. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411347-fig-0003">3e</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a> (Supporting Information) show that the NMMZ3 show almost the same discharge capacities of 119.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with different charge current densities. Using the highest charge current density of 20 C rate, with a CC+ CV (4.3&nbsp;V) mode, the total charge time is 14.2 min (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12d</a>, Supporting Information). It is clear that the NNMZ3 can be fully charged in less than 15 min. The NMMZ3 cathode shows excellent high rate performance that can satisfy high power requirement in practical application. The excellent rate performance of NMMZ3 material should be attributed to its superior Na transport kinetics. GITT was performed to evaluate the Na transport kinetics of NMM and NMMZ3. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a> (Supporting Information), the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients (D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup>) of NMMZ3 during charge/ discharge process is 1.02E<sup>−13</sup>-3.69E<sup>−10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> which is higher than that of NMM. Zr-substitution exhibit positive effect on Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion due to the expanded sodium-ion diffusion channel.</p>
            
            <p>Long-term cycling performance is critical for the practical application of cathode materials. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411347-fig-0003">3f</a> the capacity retention of NMM and NMMZ3 after 100 cycles at 0.1 C are 83.4% and 99.1%, respectively. The detailed charge/discharge profiles are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> (Supporting Information). It can be seen that the discharge profile of NMM cathode showed more severe degradation than that of NMMZ3 cathode. The NMMZ3 cathode only degraded slightly with midpoint discharge voltage decreasing from 3.62 to 3.56&nbsp;V after 100 cycles. However, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411347-fig-0003">3f</a> shows that the discharge capacity of NMMZ3 cathode showed a slight parabolic increase over cycling in the first 47 cycles because of slightly increased capacity due to the reduction of MnO<sub>x</sub> (side reaction product on the particle surface) at the low voltage range ≈2&nbsp;V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0031_R_d11372999e1162" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Power Sources">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> NMMZ3 also displays better long-term cycle stability at 1 C, with high reversible capacity of 116.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and capacity retention of 84.2% after 500 cycles. In contrast, NMM exhibits rapid capacity decay with capacity retention of 60% after 200 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411347-fig-0003">3g</a>). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411347-fig-0003">3h,i</a> shows the charge/discharge profiles of NMM and NMMZ3 at 1 C for 1st, 50th, 100th, 200th cycles, respectively. It can be seen that NMM cathode exhibited greater voltage fading compared with NMMZ3 cathode and showed lower initial discharge voltage at all cycles. It is obvious that Mg/Zr co-substitution greatly enhances the electrochemical performance of P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, and the cycling stability and rate property are top-ranked among P2-type sodium layered cathodes reported so far (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). The long-term cycling stability of the materials is closely related to their chemical structure and surface/electrolyte situations.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202411347-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202411347-sec-0050-title">2.3 Structural Evolution and the Phase Transition</h3>
            
            <p>To get insight to the essential function mechanism of Mg/Zr co-substitution on the structure stability of NMMZ3 upon the Na<sup>+</sup> extraction/insertion process, we compared the structural evolutions of the NMM and NMMZ3 electrodes via in situ XRD analyses carried out at 0.1 C in the voltage ranges of 2–4.3&nbsp;V (first cycle) and 2–4.6&nbsp;V (second cycle), respectively. The 2D counter maps of (002) peaks of NMM and NMMZ3 are shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202411347-fig-0004">4a,b</a>, and the detailed in situ XRD patterns are displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a> (Supporting Information), respectively. It can be seen that the peaks’ changing trends of NMM and NMMZ3 are similar both in 2–4.3 and 2–4.6&nbsp;V. During the charging process, the (002) and (004) peaks associated with the lattice parameter c shift to a lower angle, while the (100), (012) peaks related to the lattice parameter a shift to higher angles, indicating the c-axis expansion with the extraction of sodium ions and the <i>ab</i> plane contraction due to the decreased size of TM ions with higher oxidation states.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0032_R_d11372999e1210" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> When charged above 4.2&nbsp;V, the (002) diffraction peaks gradually shift back to a higher angle due to the slipping of TMO<sub>2</sub> layers and forming a new OP4 phase, which is an intergrowth structure of P2 and O2 phases. The (002) peaks become broader and less intense indicating the interruption of the long-range ordering of the pristine P2 structure. In the discharge process, the OP4 phase recovers to the P2 phase when discharged to 3.5&nbsp;V, and then the characteristic P2-phase shift to the direction converse to charging process until it returns to the initial state. Therefore, the phase change during charge/ discharge can be regarded as a reversible process of P2-OP4-P2 for both NMM and NMMZ3. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411347-fig-0004">4a,b</a> obviously shows that the (002) peak shift of NMMZ3 cathode during the phase transition from P2 phase to OP4 phase is lower than NMM cathode both in the 2.0–4.3 and 2.0-4.6&nbsp;V ranges, indicating the less formation of O2 stacking and more preserve of P2 stacking in the intergrowth structure of OP4 phase. As the O2 phase transformation induces severe strain in the structure, the alleviated phase change of NMMZ3 may reduce internal lattice stresses and suppress cracks after prolonged cycling. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411347-fig-0004">4c,d</a> display the SEM morphologies of surface particles, and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16a,b</a> (Supporting Information) show the cross-sectional images of the NMM cathode (after 200 cycles) and NMMZ3 cathode (after 500 cycles), respectively. The particle cracking was observed for NMM electrode. In contrast, the NMMZ3 particles show good particle integrity, and no obvious intergranular fracture was found in both the surface particles image and the cross-sectional SEM image. The excellent structure stability of NMMZ3 sample can be further verified by the XRD results of the cycled NMM and NMMZ3 electrodes (200 cycles at 1 C) as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a> (Supporting Information). The results of Rietveld refinement on the XRD patterns before and after 200 cycles are shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information). It was found that the variation of lattice parameter a, c, and volume for NMM are 0.06%, 0.7%, and 0.81%, respectively. In comparison, the variation of lattice parameter a, c, and cell volume for NMMZ3 are as low as 0.06%, 0.2%, and 0.38%, respectively. The good preservation of pristine lattice parameters of NMMZ3 can be attributed to the Zr pinning effect that suppresses the unfavorable slab sliding during charge/discharge process. The more severe volume variation leads to the cracking of NMM particles. Particle cracking also causes more fresh surface exposed to the electrolyte, which increases the side reaction and dissolution of transition metals as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411347-fig-0004">4e</a>, and ultimately leads to a rapid decline in the capacity of the material. What's more, the surface of NMM particles obviously becomes rough owing to the by-product of side reactions (see Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411347-fig-0004">4c</a>). In contrast, the cycled NMMZ3 cathode kept a smooth surface and good particle integrity without cracks even after 500 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411347-fig-0004">4d</a>), and the TM dissolution is much less than that of NMM (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411347-fig-0004">4e</a>).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202411347-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/28132ce3-8647-4413-a1ec-d4ca93665833/adfm202411347-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/28132ce3-8647-4413-a1ec-d4ca93665833/adfm202411347-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/75c5d572-a5c3-4b9d-b090-8b9ce215efd9/adfm202411347-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/28132ce3-8647-4413-a1ec-d4ca93665833/adfm202411347-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202411347-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202411347" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a,b) Galvanostatic charge–discharge curves, magnified (002) reflection for the primary two cycles obtained from in-operando XRD in 2–4.3&nbsp;V/4.6&nbsp;V and corresponding contour plot of NMM and NMMZ3. c) SEM image of NMM electrode after 200 cycles at 1 C. d) SEM image of NMMZ3 electrode after 500 cycles at 1 C. e) ICP measurement of Ni, Mn dissolution of NMM and NMMZ3 cathodes. f) Calculated oxygen vacancy formation energy of NMM and NMMZ3.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The surface chemical composition of the cycled electrodes was detected by XPS. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a> (Supporting Information) shows that the cycled NMMZ3 displayed relatively low intensity peaks of the organic and inorganic components originating from electrolyte decomposition, indicating that the existence of ZrO<sub>2</sub> surface layer protected the NMMZ3 material surface from electrolyte erosion during long-term cycling process. The robust Zr-pinning layer structure combining with the protective Zr─O shell contribute to its long cycling life. The cycling stability of NMMZ3 cathode in a wide voltage range of 2–4.6&nbsp;V has also been tested in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a> (Supporting Information). It is amazing that the NMMZ3 cathode still exhibited a high capacity retention of 87.9% after 350 cycles with the initial capacity of 127.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. Increasing the upper charging voltage to 4.6&nbsp;V means more Na<sup>+</sup> will be removed from the sodium layer and more severe electrolyte decomposition, which is a huge challenge for the structure stability of cathode materials. The NMMZ3 displays such a better cycling stability in 2–4.6&nbsp;V further demonstrate the highly effective function of Zr<sup>4+</sup>as pins to maintain the layer structure and ZrO<sub>2</sub> coating layer preventing electrolyte attack during charge/discharge process. All the above results indicate that the NMMZ3 is a promising cathode material for long life and fast charge and discharge sodium-ion batteries.</p>
            
            <p>Finally, it is known that anionic oxygen redox though provide extra capacity during high voltage cycling it also leads to the triggering of a degradation mechanism, which is related to the oxygen loss and aggrevated electrolyte consumption.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0033_R_d11372999e1287" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Nat. Mater. Chem. Mater. Nat. Nanotechnol.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To further explain the observed ultrastable high voltage cycling stability of NMMZ3 sample, the formation energy of an oxygen vacancy of the NMM and NMMZ3 is calculated, respectively. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411347-fig-0004">4f</a>. the NMM shows a lower oxygen vacancy formation energy than NMMZ3, which demonstrates that Zr-substitution can suppress the oxygen vacancy formation. The multifunction of Zr-substitution contributes to the excellent charge/discharge reversibility.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202411347-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202411347-sec-0060-title">2.4 All-Climate Property, Air Stability and Thermal Stability</h3>
            
            <p>The outdoor temperature in most areas is usually between −20 and 45&nbsp;°C. Herein, we perform an all-climate assessment on the cells from −25 to 55&nbsp;°C. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202411347-fig-0005">5a</a>, <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a> (Supporting Information), successive temperature changing did not affect the normal operation of the cell. When the temperature drops to very low temperature, the electrolyte remains liquid state (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information). However, the viscosity of the electrolyte and the impedance of the electrode/electrolyte interface increase, and the ionic conductivity decreases,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0034_R_d11372999e1316" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Chem. Commun.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> so the capacity of both NMM and NMMZ3 decreases. The NMMZ3 cell delivered a higher capacity than NMM regardless of high/low temperature. Even at −25&nbsp;°C, it can deliver a capacity of 81.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with energy density of 330&nbsp;Wh Kg<sup>−1</sup> (for cathode material), indicating a fast Na<sup>+</sup> kinetics of NMMZ3. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411347-fig-0005">5b,c</a> further shows the cycling performance of NMM and NMMZ3 at −5 and 55&nbsp;°C, respectively. The NMMZ3 cell displayed an initial capacity of 103.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1C, −5&nbsp;°C, superior to the 88.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> of NNM cell. When the temperature was increased to 55&nbsp;°C, the NMM and NMMZ3 showed initial capacities of 114.6 and 120.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, and with capacity retention of 47.1% and 79% after 200 cycles, respectively. Compared with NMM, NMMZ3 shows better cycling stability in a wide temperature range (−25–55&nbsp;°C). The above results show that NMMZ3 is a promising cathode material for all-climate sodium-ion batteries.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202411347-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5e894f6c-b821-4529-9dea-b6235eeb4e4c/adfm202411347-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/5e894f6c-b821-4529-9dea-b6235eeb4e4c/adfm202411347-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/80ab83c3-d550-4e64-bafd-f0e2de3f001d/adfm202411347-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5e894f6c-b821-4529-9dea-b6235eeb4e4c/adfm202411347-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202411347-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202411347" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Discharge capacities of NMM and NMMZ3 at different temperatures. b,c) Cycling performance of NMM and NMMZ3 at −5 and 55&nbsp;°C. d, e) Initial charge/discharge profiles and cycling performance of NMMZ3 cathodes before and after the moisture exposure. f) DSC curves of NMM and NMMZ3 cathodes.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The cathode materials with moisture sensitivity may greatly affect their storage and electrode manufacture.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0011_R_d11372999e1360" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Energy Environ. Sci. ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Fortunately, NMMZ3 exhibits excellent humid immunity. An experiment as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a> (Supporting Information) was arranged to evaluate the humid sensitivity of the NMM and NMMZ3 materials. The prepared sample electrodes were exposed to 60% RH humidity environment for two weeks, and then the cell was assembled after drying in a vacuum oven for 12&nbsp;h. The NMM materials delivered poor electrochemical performance after exposure experiment (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a>, Supporting Information). In comparison, it is found that the first charge and discharge profiles of NMMZ3 before and after exposure are completely overlapping (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411347-fig-0005">5d</a>), and the cycle stability is still well maintained after exposure experiment (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411347-fig-0005">5e</a>). DFT calculation results (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a>, Supporting Information) show that NMMZ3 displays a high surface adsorption energy for water molecules which well explains the excellent humid-resistant property of this material.</p>
            
            <p>DSC was carried out to evaluate the thermal stability of the NMM and NMMZ3 materials after charging to 4.3&nbsp;V. Similar to other layered oxide materials, the DSC curves display two exothermic reaction peaks between 200 and 300&nbsp;°C in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202411347-fig-0005">5f</a>. The first peak represents the reaction between the material and the electrolyte, and the second peak represents the structural transformation of the material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0032_R_d11372999e1387" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">32</a>, <a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0035_R_d11372999e1387" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The thermal decomposition peak positions of the charged NMMZ3 are obviously higher than those of charged NMM, and the heat generation of charged NMMZ3 is lower than that of charged NMM. These results exhibit that high Zr─O binding energy and Zr─O shell on the particle surface can enhance the thermal stability of the modified material.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202411347-sec-0070">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202411347-sec-0070-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we propose a combined structural and surface modulation strategy to improve high-voltage cycling stability of P2-Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> using Mg and Zr as the substitution elements. The prepared material Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.25</sub>Mg<sub>0.08</sub>Mn<sub>0.64</sub>Zr<sub>0.03</sub>O<sub>2</sub> delivers a high specific capacity of 123.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C and 105.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 20 C, which can be fully charged in less than 15&nbsp;min and discharged in 2.5 min. What's more, it exhibits a high-capacity retention of 84.2% after 500 cycles at 1 C in 2–4.3&nbsp;V, and 87.9% after 350 cycles at 1 C in 2–4.6&nbsp;V. The remarkably electrochemical performance can be ascribed to the following aspects: 1) Mg substitution suppress P2-O2 phase transition by forming a OP4 phase and Zr substitution further improves the reversibility of P2-OP4 phase transition by the pinning effect and strong Zr─O bonds; 2) Zr substitution enlarges the Na<sup>+</sup> transport channels, leading to the enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics; 3) Zr substitution restrains the oxygen loss by increasing the formation energy of oxygen vacancies; 4) Zr─O protective layer formed during the calcination process protects the materials’ surface from electrolyte attacking. This work provides a practicable tactic to achieve high-stable and high-power cathode material through double elements co-substitution.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202411347-sec-0080">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202411347-sec-0080-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202411347-sec-0090">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202411347-sec-0100">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202411347-sec-0110">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202411347-sec-0110-title"> Synthesis of Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.25</sub>Mg<sub>0.08</sub>Mn<sub>0.67-x/100</sub>Zr<sub>x/100</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (x = 0, 1, 3, 5) Compounds</h4>
                  
                  <p>A series of [Ni<sub>0.25</sub>Mg<sub>0.08</sub>Mn<sub>0.67-x/100</sub>Zr<sub>x/100</sub>]CO<sub>3</sub> (x = 0, 1, 3, 5) precursors were synthesized via a co-precipitation method. Typically, a stoichiometric mixture of NiSO<sub>4</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O, MnSO<sub>4</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O, MgSO<sub>4</sub>·7H<sub>2</sub>O, and Zr(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·4H<sub>2</sub>O was dissolved in deionized water to obtain a 2&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> sulfate solution. Then 2&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> precipitant solution and the 2&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> sulfate solution were simultaneously and separately pumped into a four-necked flask at the same feeding rate. a desired amount of NH<sub>3</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O solution was also pumped into the reactor. The pH value (≈10.6) and temperature (≈60&nbsp;°C) of the reaction solution as well as the stirring speed (350 rmp) were carefully controlled. The whole reaction process was carried out in a N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere. The final obtained precipitate was filtered and washed with deionized water until the pH value of the filtered water was ≈7.0 followed by drying at 120&nbsp;°C for 15&nbsp;h.</p>
                  
                  <p>The obtained precursor powders were stoichiometrically mixed with Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, respectively, by ball milling and an excess of 3wt. %Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> was added to compensate sodium loss at high temperature. The mixtures were first calcined at 500&nbsp;°C for 5&nbsp;h to ensure the precursor fully decomposed and then calcined at 900&nbsp;°C for 15&nbsp;h. After naturally cooling, all samples were ground and screened through a 200-mesh sieve to obtain the final Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.25</sub>Mg<sub>0.08</sub>Mn<sub>0.67-x/100</sub>Zr<sub>x/100</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (simply denoted as NMMZx) (x&nbsp;=&nbsp;0, 1, 3, 5) products.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202411347-sec-0120">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202411347-sec-0120-title"> Materials Characterization—Physical Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>The structural characteristic of the as-prepared materials was determined by powder X-ray diffraction using Bruker D8 Advance X-ray diffractometer (XRD) with Cu Kα radiation (λ = 0.154056&nbsp;nm). The high-intensity XRD patterns were collected at a scan rate of 3 s per step and increment of 0.031°. For the in situ XRD measurement, the time per step and increment were 0.3 s and 0.031°, respectively. An in situ battery mold with Be window was used. The morphology of the samples was observed by Field emission scanning electron microscopy (Nova NanoSEM 450 FEI Company). TEM and EDXS were performed using an aberration-corrected JEOL JEM-ARM 200CF STEM equipped with a 200&nbsp;keV cold-field emission gun, with an HAADF detector and an Oxford X-max 80 SDD X-ray detector. The soft X-ray absorption spectrum(sXAS)was recorded using a total electron yield (TEY) mode, and the photon flux of the incident beam (I0) was monitored simultaneously based on the photocurrent of the Au grid located before the entrance of the end station under vacuum conditions of 5&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−9</sup>&nbsp;torr. For DSC testing, the cathode materials were charged to 4.3&nbsp;V and then scraped off from the current collectors after washing with DMC. The scraped materials and electrolyte were heated from 40 to 300&nbsp;°C with a heating rate of 10&nbsp;°C&nbsp;min<sup>−1</sup>.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202411347-sec-0130">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202411347-sec-0130-title"> Materials Characterization—Electrochemical testing</h4>
                  
                  <div class="paragraph-element">The cathodes were prepared by mixing 80&nbsp;wt. % active material, 10&nbsp;wt. % super P, and 10&nbsp;wt. % PVDF in N-methyl-2-pyrrolidinone (NMP) solvent followed by coating the mixed slurry on Al foil. After drying at 80&nbsp;°C for ≈2&nbsp;h to evaporate the NMP, the electrode disks were punched (ɸ12 mm) and dried at 120&nbsp;°C for 12&nbsp;h in a vacuum oven. The mass loading of active material was ≈2.5–3&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>. The cathodes were incorporated into coin cells (R2016) with sodium metal foil as anode and 1&nbsp;M NaPF<sub>6</sub>/PC + EMC (2:3&nbsp;vol.%) + 2&nbsp;wt.%FEC + 2&nbsp;wt.%PST (prop-1-ene-1,3-sultone) electrolyte in an argon-filled glove box (H<sub>2</sub>O&nbsp;&lt; 0.1, O<sub>2</sub>&nbsp;&lt; 0.1&nbsp;ppm). Galvanostatic charge–discharge cycling tests for the cells were performed between 2.0&nbsp;V and 4.3/4.6&nbsp;V in a wide temperature range (−25 to 55&nbsp;°C) using a battery test system (Land CT2001A model, Wuhan Jinnuo Electronics Co., Ltd). Cyclic voltammetry (CV) was carried out at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV/s using a CHI electrochemical workstation (CHI 604D, CHI Instrument Co.). For galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) measurements, cells were charged/discharge at 13&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> for 10 min, followed by relaxing for 1&nbsp;h. Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients were calculated according to the following equation:

                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202411347-sec-0140">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202411347-sec-0140-title"> DFT Calculations</h4>
                  
                  <p>DFT calculations were performed with the Vienna Ab-initio Simulation Package (VASP), using the Generalized Gradient Approximation (GGA) with Perdew Burke Ernzerhof (PBE) functional method and appling an energy cut-off of 500&nbsp;eV. The U value for Ni, Mn, Zr ions was 6.1, 3.8, and 5&nbsp;eV, respectively. The energy and force convergence limits were 10<sup>−6</sup>&nbsp;eV and 0.02&nbsp;eV Å<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The calculations were performed on 3 × 3 × 2 supercells and the vaccum slab was 15 Å.</p>

Funct. Mater.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>

Similarly, E(Mg<sub>bulk</sub>), E(Ni<sub>bulk</sub>), E(Mn<sub>bulk</sub>), and E(Zr<sub>bulk</sub>) is derived from metallic Mg, Ni, Mn, and Zr crystal, and E(O<sub>2</sub>) is the energy of O<sub>2</sub> gas.</p>

                  <div class="paragraph-element">The formation energy of oxygen vacancies can be obtained by the following formula:<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202411347-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202411347-bib-0033_R_d11372999e1942" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Nat. Mater. Chem. Mater. Nat. Nanotechnol.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202411347-disp-0004"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                           <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/30553a94-bdff-49b9-b15f-ca65b0b354e3/adfm202411347-math-0006.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202411347:adfm202411347-math-0006" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202411347-math-0006.png">
                              <semantics>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <msub>
                                       <mi>E</mi>
                                       <mi>Ov</mi>
                                    </msub>
                                    <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                                    <msub>
                                       <mi>E</mi>
                                       <mi>total</mi>
                                    </msub>
                                    <mo linebreak="goodbreak">−</mo>
                                    <mi>E</mi>
                                    <mo linebreak="goodbreak">+</mo>
                                    <msub>
                                       <mi>u</mi>
                                       <mi mathvariant="normal">o</mi>
                                    </msub>
                                 </mrow>
                                 <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{{E}_{{\mathrm{Ov}}}} = {{E}_{{\mathrm{total}}}} - E + {{u}_{\mathrm{o}}}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                              </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(4)</span></div>where E<sub>total</sub> is the total energy of sample structure, E is the energy of the structure containing O<sup>−</sup> holes and u<sub>o</sub> is the chemical potential of the O atom.</div>

         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202411347-sec-0150-title"> Acknowledgements</h2>
         
         <p>The authors gratefully acknowledge financial support from the National Natural Science Foundation of China (No. 21938005 and 21573147) and the Natural Science Foundation of Fujian Province of China (2020J01422). The authors also thank beamline BL20U2 of SSRF (Shanghai Synchrotron Radiation Facility) for providing beam time.</p>
         
         <ol></ol>
      </div>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202411347-sec-0160">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202411347-sec-0160-title"> Conflict of Interest</h2>
         
         <p>The authors declare no conflict of interest.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202411347-sec-0170">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202411347-sec-0170-title"> Author Contributions</h2>
         
         <p>S.Y. and X.-Z.L. conceived the original idea, designed the experiments wrote and revised the manuscript. S.Y. synthesized the samples and performed the characterizations and electrochemical measurements. S.D. conducted DFT calculation. J.M. performed Cross-sectional SEM characterization. Q.Z. and Y.Z. carried out in situ XRD. S.Y., X.B., Q.L., G.C., J.-W.W., C.Q., and X.-Z.L. analyzed the results. L.Y. performed the HRTEM measurements. All authors discussed the results and reviewed the manuscript.</p>
         </section>
